01
Money-makers · 7-filter methodology
Best trades today VALID
🔥 Today's high-conviction trades
Thesis & methodology
RegimeNEUTRALTop sectorXLVBottom sectorXLCTrades5L / 0S
Today's read. The macro regime is NEUTRAL (no extreme). Constructive: regime allows long exposure with tight stops.

Why 0 shorts and 5 longs? All directions equally weighted — but final conviction depends on confluence + sector + options layer.

Methodology recap (7 filters cascaded).
1. Macro regime — EUPHORIA/RISK-OFF/RISK-ON drives bias preference.
2. Sector strength — RS 20d vs SPY, top/bottom 3 alignment.
3. Ticker quality — market cap > $2B, liquidity > $5M/day, anti-blow-off, anti-falling-knife.
4. Cross-screener confluence — minimum 3 screeners in the cockpit firing simultaneously.
5. Options layer — IV/HV ratio + OI-PCR + super-euphoria + smart-money flow.
6. Setup timing — anti-pump, anti-knife, reversal candle for LONG, topping pattern for SHORT.
6.5. Catalyst calendar — earnings DTE adjustments (skip event risk on LONG, straddle on IV ramp).
7. R:R minimum 2:1 — ATR-based stop/target, skip if R:R < 2.

Honest assessment. These setups passed all 7 filters but the market context still requires risk management. Cross-check earnings calendar AND chart 6-12 months for each name before sizing up.

02
Macro play · 5-step rotation methodology
Rotation play — long laggards (equity + calls) VALID
🔄 Rotation play methodology — long laggards, no shorts, no options-sell
5-step rotation methodology
LeaderXLKDecorr laggards7Quality picks64
The methodology (5 steps).

Step 1 — Identify decorrelated laggards. Compute 30-day correlation of each sector ETF to the leader (XLK). Keep sectors with correlation < +0.4 (decoupled) AND RS 20d < 0 (laggard vs SPY). Don't bet on a sector unless it's BOTH decoupled AND already lagging — otherwise you're just adding correlated risk.

Step 2 — Filter quality tickers in those sectors. For each decorr-laggard sector, pull the SP500 names. Apply hard filters: market cap > $5B, price > 92% of MA200 (not broken), individual 30d correlation to leader < +0.4, RSI 35-65 (balanced — not capitulation, not euphoric).

Step 3 — Confirm momentum. Require positive relative strength: ticker's 5-day return − SPY's 5-day return > -3% (i.e., not still falling vs market). This filters out laggards that are still getting weaker (no edge in catching falling knife). We want sectors that have started showing relative strength.

Step 4 — Entry timing. Three valid entry triggers:
(a) Pullback to MA20 in confirmed sector uptrend
(b) Breakout above 20-day high on rising volume
(c) Base-building 4+ weeks then breakout
Don't enter a name just because it's decorrelated — wait for the price action to confirm.

Step 5 — Position sizing & exit. Sizing 2-3% portfolio per ticker. Stop = -2.5×ATR(14) or MA50 break. Target 1 = +15% (typical sector rotation gain over 3 months). Target 2 = MA200 reclaim or +25%. Horizon 3-6 months. Trail stop after +10% to protect gains. Sector rotation plays are slow — don't day-trade them, don't double-up on a single name.

Top blow-off candidate today: TXN (score 6, target ~$260.25 (DD20d -11.6%)).

8 ticker(s) fire at least ONE Bonferroni-validated backtested signal (25y, 50,109 obs, p<0.001). 1 stack ≥3 points = high conviction.

Methodology: the backtest-validated signals measure max drawdown over 20d (not the mean forward return — masked by rebounds in a bull market). The academic heuristics (Greenwood-Shleifer-You, Edwards-Magee, gap, island, LPPLS) stay displayed for context but do not survive Bonferroni on their own — they only confirm.
How to read
Backtest-validated signals (25 years, 91 tickers, 50,109 observations, p<0.001):
• Two-red parabolic (weight 3) — 2 red days after parabolic. DD 20d -11.55%, lift +20pp, N=302.
• Stoch cross<80 + parabolic (weight 2) — DD 20d -9.65%, N=148.
• Rally + red on volume (weight 2) — rally 10%+ then red day. DD 20d -7.23%, N=93.
• Shooting star / BB fail / Bearish engulf (weight 1) — DD 20d -3 to -5%.

Options decision = multi-factor composite 0-100 (IV Rank · IV-HV · term struct · skew 25Δ · implied/DD ratio · liquidity). Score ≥70 + liquidity OK → 🟢 GO ATM Put. Score 50-70 → 🟡 GO LIGHT OTM 25Δ. <50 or liquidity KO → 🔴 NO GO + reason.

Global composite REJECT on its own (mean forward return bull-biased). Individual signals VALID on the max-DD metric.

Best rebound theme historically: AI Megacaps (median +8.5% on 30d, +2.0pp vs SPY).
Worst rebound theme: Bonds long (TLT) (median -0.4%, -7.0pp vs SPY).

If SPY corrects -5%: historically the best 30d rebounds are in speculative tech (AI Megacaps, AI Supply Chain, Quantum) — high beta that rebounds harder than the market. Hedges (long Bonds, Gold) are neutral over this horizon.

Medians used (not means) to avoid extreme outliers (SMR/OKLO/RGTI did +200-400% after some crashes).
How to read
Detecting SPY -5% troughs: price ≤ -5% off the rolling 20d high, then find the local minimum within the next 10 days. Minimum 30 days between two troughs (no double counting).

For each trough, we compute the forward return of every theme ticker at 30d and 60d. We aggregate by median (= typical rebound of an average theme name, not the mean dragged by outliers).

Lift vs SPY = (theme median − SPY median). Lift >+4pp = STRONG REBOUND, 1-4pp = DECENT, -1 to +1 = NEUTRAL, -3 to -1 = LAGS, <-3pp = HEDGE/DIVERGES.

Practical read: if SPY drops -5% tomorrow, wait 5-10d for the bottom to form, then allocate first to STRONG REBOUND themes (high beta tech). Complement with hedges (Bonds, Gold) if the drawdown deepens past -10%+.

A dispersion ≥ 25pp = late-cycle warning (Oct 2007, Dec 1999, Jan 2022 had similar readings). When a single sector carries the market, the correction is violent and the oversold laggards (-10pp+) are the priority rebound candidates once the rotation kicks in.
How to read
RS 20d = sector 20d ret − SPY 20d ret. Measures relative leadership.
Verdicts:
• LEADER (red, RS ≥ +8pp) — dominates the market. Risk = blow-off / mean-reversion.
• STRONG (red, +2 to +8pp) — outperforms.
• INLINE — RS -2 to +2pp, moves with the market.
• LAGGING (green, -7 to -2pp) — slightly underperforms.
• WEAK (green, -12 to -7pp) — falling behind.
• OVERSOLD (green dark, ≤ -12pp) — extreme breakdown, mean-revert candidate.

Dispersion 20d = leader RS − laggard RS. <10pp = broad rally, 10-18pp = healthy trending, 18-25pp = late-cycle, ≥25pp = end-cycle warning.

Tactical use: if a long stock passes your filter but its sub-sector is WEAK, headwind. If STRONG, a rising tide confirms the setup. When a GICS level-1 sector (Energy) is mixed, the level 2-3 drilldown (Oil & Gas E&P vs Refining vs Equipment) reveals which sub-sector carries the move.
How to read
Verdict = 6-metric composite score: %>MA50 ≥60 (+1) or ≤40 (-1) · %>MA200 ≥50 (+1) or ≤30 (-1) · RS 20d ≥+1pp (+1) or ≤-1pp (-1) · RS 60d ≥+2pp (+1) or ≤-2pp (-1) · Med ret 20d ≥+2% (+1) or ≤-2% (-1) · A/D ≥+20 (+1) or ≤-20 (-1). STRONG if score ≥+3, WEAK if ≤-3, otherwise NEUTRAL.

RS (Relative Strength) = (sub-sector median return) − (SPY return) in percentage points. A/D = (N up − N down) / N total × 100 over 20d. %>MA50/200 = classic CANSLIM/Minervini breadth.

Universe: 3887 GICS-classified tickers (industries ≥5 tickers only). The missing 45% (FMP backfill TODO) are mostly illiquid small caps.

• ≥65 STRONG (RISK-ON, normal sizing)
• 50-65 DECENT (NEUTRAL, selective)
• 35-50 WEAK (CAUTION, trim + hedge)
• 20-35 VERY WEAK (RISK-OFF, cash 40-50%)
• <20 CAPITULATION (BTFD setup, scale-in starts)

G8 currencies equal-weighted index · D1 / W / M ranking · z-normalised · RISK-OFF Bonferroni-VALID 11y
FX MTF Strength — USD · EUR · GBP · JPY · CHF · CAD · AUD · NZD relative strength meter VALID
📅 As of 2026-06-05 · 307 bars cached · 8 G8 currencies × equal-weighted index vs the 7 others · z-score normalised on 252d rolling window
RankCcyD1 %W %M %D1 zW zM zComposite z
#1USD0.69%1.45%1.92%2.442.011.441.96
#2JPY0.53%0.84%0.22%1.451.310.981.25
#3CAD0.39%0.23%-0.07%1.860.53-0.000.79
#4GBP0.00%0.51%0.24%0.011.000.330.45
#5EUR-0.12%0.26%-0.13%-0.320.59-0.120.05
#6CHF-0.06%-0.62%-0.21%-0.21-1.31-0.56-0.69
#7NZD-0.74%-2.04%-0.63%-1.91-2.68-0.40-1.66
#8AUD-0.68%-0.63%-1.34%-1.88-1.31-2.09-1.76
**Verdict: RISK-OFF** — #1 USD (z=+1.96) · #8 AUD (z=-1.76). Top: USD (composite z=+1.96). Bottom: AUD (composite z=-1.76). Cross-asset reading: USD bid + JPY bid → risk-off, USD low + JPY low → risk-on, USD bid + JPY low → carry trade (risk-on funded in yen), USD low + JPY bid → JPY flight.
How to read
**Method**: for each currency X in the G8 basket (USD, EUR, GBP, JPY, CHF, CAD, AUD, NZD), we compute an equal-weighted index = mean of the log-returns log(X/Y) vs the 7 other currencies Y. On each timeframe (D1=1d, W=5d, M=21d), we cumulate then normalize by the 252d rolling std → z-score. Composite z = equal-weighted mean of the 3 z. Rank by composite descending (1 = strongest currency). **Backtest 11y (2015-2026, MCPT 5000× + Bonferroni n=15) — VALID**: RISK-OFF (USD+JPY both bid, N=203 events) → SPY excess return **+0.53%/5d** (p_bonf=0.027 ✅) and **+0.73%/10d** (p_bonf=0.036 ✅). Contrarian pattern ('buy the panic') confirmed. **Walk-forward train 2015-2022 / test 2023-2026**: the signal **improves OOS** (test 5d +0.88% vs train +0.53%, test 10d +1.02% vs train +0.78%) — no overfit. All other regimes (RISK_ON, CARRY_TRADE, JPY_FLIGHT, USD_BID_SOLO) REJECT at p_bonf < 0.05. **Usage**: RISK-OFF active → don't short naively, wait 5-10d for the technical rebound (FX fear is contrarian on SPY). Consistent with BTFD / mean-reversion (BOTTOM screener, RSI Breadth CAPITULATION). Inspired by the @kerberos007 thread (Mar 26, 2020) 'Multi-Time Frame FX Currency Relative Strength Meter'.

TLT0.200.757,69638,486157,589209,454✓ smart money hedged
OI-PCR (open interest) reflects stable positioning — smart money hedging. Vol-PCR (today's volume) reflects intraday flow — often retail. Big gap = signal: Vol-PCR >> OI-PCR means panic intraday (often buy zone), Vol-PCR << OI-PCR means smart money continues hedging despite calm trading.
How to read
Vol-PCR > 1.5 = bearish flow today. OI-PCR > 1.5 = stable bearish positioning. Comparing the two distinguishes panic (transient) from real distribution (durable). Best Kerberos signal: when OI-PCR rising over weeks alongside calm Vol-PCR — hidden distribution.

Skew = wing IV (out-of-money) − ATM IV. Positive skew = puts/calls are expensive vs ATM (tail risk priced in). Negative skew = wings cheap vs ATM (rare, often pre-merger or stable utility). Top names: best candidates for skew-trade (selling expensive wings, buying cheap ones).

Kerberos · dealer positioning
GEX profile (SPY, QQQ, IWM) VALID
📅 EOD 2026-06-05 · GEX profile per strike · 3 major index ETFs
IndexSpotZero ΓMax +ΓGEX +Max -ΓGEX -
SPY752.42—762.0013.6M625.00-63.6M
QQQ730.05—725.0012.6M715.00-20.1M
IWM289.00155.00290.008.0M273.00-18.4M
DIA516.73380.00535.002.4M470.00-2.0M
Dealer gamma positioning: Zero gamma = level where dealers flip from long-gamma (stabilizing) to short-gamma (accelerating). Max +Gamma = strike where dealers most long → resistance/magnet (price tends to pin there). Max -Gamma = strike where dealers most short → if breached, acceleration zone. Use these levels for entry/exit precision on 0-7 DTE options.
How to read
GEX (gamma exposure) measures dealer hedging requirements. Above zero gamma = dealers stabilize (bull friendly), below = dealers accelerate moves (volatility regime). Max positive gamma strike acts as resistance/pinning level. Breaking max negative gamma strike = expect rapid acceleration in that direction.

Volatility · regime gold standard
VIX term structure (VX1-VX9) VALID
📅 EOD 2026-06-05 · VIX term structure · 4 contracts on the curve
ContractTenorLevel1d Δ5d Δ
VIX 9d9d23.9289.09%89.99%

VIX 3m90d21.8213.47%16.93%
VIX 6m180d23.497.31%8.75%
🟡 MIXED (1 inversion) — transitioning regime. Inversions on the front-end signal short-term stress — watch for spread to expand to longer tenors.
How to read
VIX term structure = futures curve of S&P 500 implied volatility. Contango (rising) = market pricing future as more uncertain than now (calm now). Backwardation (falling) = market pricing now as more uncertain than future (crisis now). Front-month VIX above VIX3M is the primary regime change signal Kerberos used since 2018.

Volatility · variance risk premium

📅 EOD 2026-06-05 · VIX vs realized vol on SPY 20d
MetricValue
VIX (today)21.51
HV20 SPY (annualized %)9.2%
VRP (VIX − HV20)+12.33
Percentile (1y)95%
VerdictSHORT VOL — sell premium
VRP +12.33. Implied vol overpriced vs realized — sell premium tailwind (covered calls, credit spreads, iron condors).
How to read
VRP = VIX (forward 30-day implied vol) minus HV20 (realized 20-day vol annualized). Positive 4-7 typical (vol risk premium). Above +7 = sell premium attractive. Below 0 = market underpricing realized vol, long premium has carry.

17 READY setup(s) today — top: SPY (VSA base 5d, UNUSUAL 6d, score 100.00/100). READY = VSA base ≥3d + unusual calls ≥3d + stock not yet extended (perf 20d ≤ +8%) — the state IONQ was in early April before its breakout. FORMING = only one of the two legs in place (watch). ALREADY MOVED = the move has started (perf 20d > +18% or calls very OTM).
How to read
Swing setup, not lottery — IONQ was never a lottery, it was accumulation in a base. Playable on the stock OR via 1-3 month calls for leverage (IONQ's 5x came from the calls). Mandatory cross-check before entering: weekly chart + position in the 52w range (drop if > 95% of the high), and check there's no imminent binary catalyst (earnings, FDA). VSA accumulation patterns = TESTING / NO_SUPPLY / ABSORPTION / STOPPING_VOLUME (the seller is exhausting on support).

Kerberos rule · framework
Buffer 3-day hard cliff CONCEPT
Concept3-day hard cliff buffer
Source — generic rule applied to the Max Optimism Stack and the VIX Spike Detector.
A trigger only fires the action after 3 days of confirmation in zone, and the filter is binary. Buffer tested: <3d = too many FPs, 3d = optimal (captures the COVID 5-day cluster), >3d = misses fast corrections. The hard cliff avoids gray zones where you enter/exit in a loop: either ON or OFF.
How to read
— generic rule applied to the Max Optimism Stack and the VIX Spike Detector.
16
Kerberos rule · framework
Haven filter (FP eliminator) CONCEPT
ConceptHaven filter (FP eliminator)
Source — key component of the 4-brick Max Optimism Stack.
To confirm a risk-off regime is real, we check that at least one defensive layer moves in agreement. GLD momentum 60d ≥ -2% (gold not in free fall = real haven) OR HYG/TLT momentum 30d ≤ +2% (credit/duration not outperforming if gold rallies). Eliminates 100% of the Spring 2021 FP without missing the HITs (COVID 2020 + Bear 2022 + Aug 2024 mini-crash).
How to read
— key component of the 4-brick Max Optimism Stack.
17
Kerberos rule · framework
Composite ≥ 3 signals CONCEPT
ConceptComposite ≥ 3 signals = real edge
Source — framework principle #1.
A solo signal is noise. Three or more aligning simultaneously = measurable edge. Empirically validated by live testing: 1 brick alone = noise, 2+ = real signal. Every Kerberos composite kept in the system is an aggregate of 3-5 independent sub-signals: Max Optimism (4 bricks), Composite Divergence (3), VIX Spike Detector (4 tiers), TraderMonty (5).
How to read
— framework principle #1.
18
Kerberos rule · framework
Cross-asset alignment CONCEPT
ConceptCross-asset alignment
Source — framework principle #4.
A signal isolated to a single asset class is not enough. The true stress signature = simultaneous multi-asset alignment: SPX direction + VIX/VVIX + Gold (GLD) + Bonds (TLT, HYG) + FX (DXY, EUR, JPY) + Sectors (XLU/XLP defensives vs XLY/XLF cyclicals). Applies principle #1 (composite vs solo) to cross-asset. Consistent with the 53 cross-asset ratios of the Kerberos Macro Dashboard.
How to read
— framework principle #4.

The four readings that bother me

The first thing that bothers me is the high-beta to safe-asset ratio. This is a simple gauge of how much the market is paying for risk-on versus risk-off. At Friday's close it sat at +3.22 standard deviations above its 5-year average, top 1% of the range. Six prior instances in five years, every one followed by an index lower 60 to 90 days later. Smallest pullback was 7%, largest was 33%, average around 11%. Small sample, perfect track record on direction. I would not bet a year on six observations. I will adjust this week's posture for them.

The second thing is concentration. The seven largest mega-caps returned +15.76% on a cap-weighted basis over the past sixty days while the equal-weight S&P (RSP, which gives every company the same weight) returned NEGATIVE 0.93% over the same window — meaning the median name has lost ground while the cap-weighted index made new highs. That is a 16.69 percentage point gap, the widest two-month spread we have seen this year and inside the top decile over 24 months. The flip of RSP from positive to negative just happened on Friday's close — that is the leaders carrying everything more than they were on Thursday. The historical resolution to this kind of spread is the leaders catching down to the rest, not the rest catching up to the leaders.

🎭 MEME IDEA — Distracted Boyfriend: Boyfriend = retail investor, Girlfriend = "broad market breadth", New girl = "7 mega-cap names doing +18%". Caption: "When the equal-weight returns less than a savings account but Nvidia keeps printing."

The third thing is what the volatility curve is signaling. The 3-month VIX (VIX3M) sits at 21.36 while the front-month VIX is at 18.43 after Friday's +6.8% spot move. That is a 16% premium for 3-month protection — narrowed from 21% Thursday but still positive (close to the historical median of ~12%). The curve flattening on Friday is itself information: spot caught up to longer-dated as the equal-weight rolled over. The volatility market is still pricing more risk three months out than it is right now, but the gap is closing. That historically precedes vol regime shifts, not vol regime continuations. We lean in the same direction at small size with a calendar VIX call structure that benefits if the curve flattens further or inverts.

The fourth thing is more subtle. Zoetis, the global leader in animal health, hit RSI 6.4 this week. That is a once-a-cycle event for a quality large-cap. It also shows up on nine other capitulation screens at the same time. Ten of thirty independent screens are flagging the same name. When that happens it is no longer a screen artifact, it is real institutional capitulation. The narrative behind the drawdown (softer pet ownership, slower vet spending) is real but cyclical, not structural. Pet ownership in developed markets is a secular trend. The current price implies a structural earnings cut the company has not guided to.

What contradicts my read

I want to be honest about the other side, because the other side is real.

Nine of the eleven sectors are trading above their 50-day moving average. Fresh 52-week highs continued through the week with limited new lows. The financial stress index sits in the bottom quartile of its multi-year range. The Hindenburg Omen indicator is dormant. By every standard "is the market healthy" check, the answer is yes. If you opened your terminal at 9:30 ET on Monday and looked only at the headline numbers, you would shrug and add to longs. Friday's green close on the SPX did not weaken that read.

Both readings are true at the same time. Breadth is healthy on the surface. The data underneath is stretched. They cannot both stay true forever. One has to give. My job this week is to be positioned for the resolution I think is more likely while accepting I might be wrong on timing.

My take — updated after Friday's close

I lean bear. Less early than I was on Monday. The macro readings have been pointing this direction for three weeks, and on Friday the surface started to catch up with the underground. SPY closed −1.2% (its biggest red day in nine weeks), the equal-weight S&P flipped NEGATIVE on the rolling 60d (RSP −0.93%), the VIX spiked +6.8% intraday to 18.43, the vol curve flattened (VIX3M/VIX 1.21 → 1.16), and one sector dropped out of the above-MA50 group (8/11). That is not the data crashing — it is the data doing exactly what the thesis predicted on the first day where the leaders paused. Friday confirmed the playbook in slow motion.

Bears have been wrong before. They will be wrong again. But the asymmetric trade is now slightly less asymmetric — three of the seven readings moved IN THE DIRECTION OF THE THESIS this week. That is no longer a contrarian setup against a green tape. It is a leaning into a tape that has started to bend. The position size stays small, the hedge stays on, the cash stays high, but the conviction on direction tightens.

The book this week is small short bias, hedged, with most of the capital in cash. If next week shows further confirmation (RSP keeps making lower highs, more sectors crack MA50, VIX3M/VIX ratio compresses below 1.05) I will add to the shorts. If it shows a snap-back (RSP flips positive again, VIX retraces under 16, sectors heal) I will trim shorts and re-evaluate. No commitment beyond what the next week of data confirms.

The trade I will name in public this week — Zoetis (ZTS)

ZTS is the one I would buy first if you only had the bandwidth for one trade. The setup is the cleanest capitulation footprint I have seen in months. RSI 6.4, the lowest reading in the S&P 500 right now. Drawdown over 20% from prior high. Volume above normal. Fifty-day average rolling over. Implied volatility on the options is below realized volatility, which means the options market is pricing the stock as boring while the stock is actually moving. That is a textbook setup for long-gamma plays.

The historical analog is the 2018 capitulation in healthcare quality names. Zoetis traded a similar footprint then and returned 22% in the following 90 days. Mid-2022 and early-2023 each produced similar setups in the same name with similar resolutions.

I would buy it at current price or on a small dip. I will not give you the stop, target, or sizing in the public note. That is below the paywall.

The screener scanned 12,070 names. Seven survived all five filters (multi-screen confluence, no blow-off, no binary catalyst, smart-money alignment, sector diversification). That is the funnel.

The hedge first (non-negotiable)

VIX call spread, 60 days to expiry. Long the 22 strike, short the 28 strike. July expiry. 1.5% of book.

Net debit roughly $90 per spread at current pricing. The long strike is barely above current VIX (17.3 vs 22), so you participate immediately if vol moves higher. The short strike caps the maximum payoff at 28, which keeps the cost basis modest.

Why this hedge specifically: three-month vol is pricing 21% above front month (VIX3M 20.85 vs VIX 17.26), the curve is asking for it. The 22-28 range covers the historical "moderate stress" zone. The 60-day expiry matches the historical drawdown window (peak drawdown reached day 30-60 in past instances).

Worst case: hedge expires worthless, year takes a 1.5% drag. Best case: hedge triples and offsets a 5-7% drawdown on the equity sleeve.

Opening — How long can eight stocks carry everything?

This is a market journal, not investment advice. Always size for the worst case before betting on the best one.

Ask ten traders this morning whether the market is healthy or stretched and you get ten different answers. The bull side points to breadth: three quarters of the eleven sectors above their fifty-day average, fresh new highs without matching new lows, financial stress in the bottom quartile of its multi-year range. The bear side will tell you that the high-beta to safe-asset ratio just hit the top one percent of its five-year history, that dealer positioning is buying protection sixty days out at sizes we have not seen in months, and that the eight largest mega-caps have outpaced the equal-weight index by twenty-five percentage points over sixty days. Both sides are correct. Both readings are true at the same time. That is the entire problem this week.

I have been doing this long enough to know that markets which look fine on top and stretched underneath rarely stay that way forever. One has to give. The question I have been turning over in my head all weekend is not whether the dispersion closes. It always closes. The question is whether the median catches up to the leaders, which would mean the rally broadens out and we get another six months of grinding higher, or whether the leaders catch down to the median, which would mean the eight stocks carrying the index finally take the breather their charts are begging for.

If you read this newsletter every week you know that I lean toward the second resolution this time. You also know that I am willing to be wrong about it. So this week I am defensive but I am not aggressive about being defensive. The difference matters. A trader who is defensive trims the leaders, hedges the book, and keeps dry powder for the rotation. A trader who is aggressive about being defensive shorts the index outright, declares a top, and ends up explaining the loss to himself for the next three months. I would rather be the first one. I have tried being the second one. It does not pay.

I should also say I might be wrong about this whole read. The macro signals on my dashboard are pointing one way and I have built a book around them, but the historical sample is six observations. I have learned to be humble about samples that small. What I am betting on this week is the asymmetry, not the certainty. If I am wrong, the cost is one or two percent on the year. If I am right, the position offsets a five to seven percent drawdown on the equity sleeve.

I keep going back and forth on this all weekend. The breadth side has the better optics, the macro side has the better history. I am leaning macro this week. I would not bet a year on it.

The point of writing a weekly newsletter on this stuff is not to be right about the macro every single week. The point is to be honest about what the data is telling me and to size positions in proportion to my conviction. The traders who blow up are not the ones who have wrong views. They are the ones who size their wrong views as if they were right views. The discipline is in the sizing, not in the conviction.

So when I tell you this week that I am defensive, what I really mean is that the macro signals on my dashboard are far enough into extreme territory that I am willing to take a small short bias and a small hedge cost in exchange for the optionality of being positioned for the historical resolution. If the rotation does not arrive, the cost of the position is one or two percent on the year. If the rotation does arrive, the position offsets a five to seven percent drawdown on the equity sleeve. That is the asymmetry I am paying for. The asymmetry is real even if the timing is uncertain.

Why this rally has merit (the bull case I take seriously)

Let me start with the side I lean against, because the bull case is real and any argument that pretends otherwise is dishonest. The companies driving this rally are not the speculative shells of 2000. They are some of the most profitable enterprises in the history of capitalism. Their cash flows are growing. Their margins are expanding. The technology they sell is genuinely changing how people work, and the productivity gains being attributed to artificial intelligence are starting to show up in the actual operating numbers, not just in the slide decks.

The other thing the bulls have right is that the broad market is not as stretched as the financial press says it is. If you strip out the eight largest mega-caps and look at the equal-weight index, you find a market that has barely participated in the rally. The median stock is up less than one percent over the past sixty days. That is not a euphoric tape. By that measure, the broad market is actually undervalued relative to its own recent history, and a rotation that brings the median up to meet the leaders would be a profitable thing to be positioned for.

So why am I not in the bull camp this week? Because the historical resolution to this kind of dispersion is rarely the median catching up. The historical resolution is the leaders catching down. The reason is mechanical. Passive flows and momentum strategies create a self-reinforcing loop. Index funds buy more of what is already big. Momentum strategies buy more of what is already going up. The bigger and more up a stock gets, the more mechanical buying it attracts. That loop continues until something breaks the inflow or until the underlying earnings stop justifying the multiple expansion. When the loop breaks, the same mechanics work in reverse. Passive funds sell what gets smaller. Momentum strategies sell what stops going up. The leaders catch down faster than the median can catch up.

This is not a prediction that the loop breaks tomorrow. The loop has continued longer than any reasonable observer expected. It can continue longer still. What I am saying is that when a setup is mechanical in nature, the resolution is also mechanical, and the historical evidence on how that mechanical resolution plays out is one-sided enough to position for it.

The four readings that have me nervous this week

I run a dashboard that watches about thirty independent signals across price, volume, volatility, sentiment, and cross-asset positioning. Most weeks two or three of these signals are flagging something interesting and the rest are quiet. This week, four signals moved into extreme zones at the same time. That is the most I have seen in eight weeks. None of them are headline news. All four describe a market that looks calm at the index level and stretched underneath.

The first reading is the high-beta to safe-asset ratio. This is a simple gauge that measures how much the market is paying for risk-on exposure relative to defensive exposure. It just hit three point two three standard deviations above its five-year mean — top one percent of the entire range. This reading has been seen exactly six times in the past sixty months. February 2020, August 2020, October 2021, March 2024, July 2024, March 2025. Every single one of those six instances was followed by an index lower sixty to ninety days later. Smallest pullback was minus seven percent, largest minus thirty-three (COVID), average around minus eleven. Six observations is not a large sample. Six for six on direction is hard to argue with.

The second reading is concentration, which I covered above. The eight mega-caps returned plus twenty-six point four percent over sixty days while the equal-weight S&P returned plus zero point eight percent. Twenty-five point six percentage points of dispersion, the widest in twenty-four months.

The third reading is the most interesting because it is the least visible to retail investors. The institutional options market on the Industrial sector ETF (XLI) traded put volume yesterday equal to twelve point zero one times its standing put open interest. The ninety-day average is zero point eight five. The previous ninety-day peak was three point two zero. Roughly four times the previous ninety-day high and fourteen times the average. When institutional money buys protection on a sector ETF at twelve times standing open interest, somebody large is positioning. We do not know who. We do not need to. We just lean in the same direction at small size.

What makes the XLI flow especially interesting is the timing. Industrials as a sector has been quietly underperforming the broad market for three weeks now. The relative strength reading is minus two point one percent over the trailing twenty days. The percentage of constituents above their fifty-day average has dropped to forty-eight, which is below the fifty percent threshold I use as a sector health line. So the institutional put accumulation is happening at the same time as the underlying technical deterioration. That confluence, the sector rolling over plus institutional positioning getting set up plus the put accumulation being at twelve times standing open interest, is more than any single one of those things on its own.

The fourth reading is the one that bothered me the most this weekend. Zoetis (ZTS), the global leader in animal pharmaceuticals, just hit a fourteen-day relative strength index reading of six point four. A reading below thirty is rare. A reading below twenty is very rare. A reading of six point four is a once-a-cycle event for a quality large-cap with a clean balance sheet. The same name shows up on nine other capitulation screens at the same time, including the implied versus realized volatility list and the multi-screener confluence list (ten of thirty independent screens flagging the same name). When ten of thirty screens fire on the same name, it is no longer a screen artifact. It is real institutional capitulation in a quality company.

Six observations is a small sample. Honestly I am not sure if it should change anything. What it does change is the sizing. I am willing to lose half a percent of book on a tilt I might be wrong about. I am not willing to lose five percent on it.

Breadth check — the surface story

If you only look at the breadth indicators, the market looks healthy. That is why I want to spend a moment here on what the breadth dashboard actually shows, because the answer is more nuanced than the headline number suggests.

Composite Breadth Score (10 sub-indicators on S&P 500)

Three quarters of the eleven sectors are above their fifty-day average. Eight stocks made fresh fifty-two week highs on Thursday with zero new lows. The Hindenburg Omen indicator (which fires when you have lots of new highs and lots of new lows simultaneously, signaling internal disagreement) is dormant. The McClellan Summation index sits at plus two hundred seventeen, which is a bullish bias on a longer timeframe. The Zweig Breadth Thrust signal is not currently active but the breadth has been holding above the trigger threshold consistently.

None of these readings are signaling distress. The financial stress index, which measures liquidity and credit conditions across the financial system, sits in the bottom quartile of its multi-year range. There is no plumbing problem. No banking stress. No funding crisis brewing. The credit markets are calm.

So why am I still defensive? Because breadth tells you whether the participation is broad. It does not tell you whether the leaders are stretched. The breadth was also healthy in October 2021, in March 2024, in July 2024 — three of the six prior instances when the aggression ratio went to plus three sigma. In each of those cases, the breadth held up for two to four weeks after the macro signal fired, then rolled over with the leaders. Breadth is a coincident indicator, not a leading one. It will tell you the market is fine right up until it tells you the market is broken.

I do not have a clean answer on which to trust. I have learned the hard way that breadth tends to be coincident, not leading. So when both are saying different things, I default to the leading indicator and accept I might be early. Being early on a defensive bias costs less than being late on it.

That is exactly where the institutional put flow on XLI is concentrated. Utilities sit in DEAD zone, but DEAD is contrarian-bullish in this dashboard, not bearish — names that are completely forgotten tend to mean-revert higher when the rotation starts.

The trade implication of this sector map is straightforward. Be small short on the sectors that are EUPHORIC. Be small long on the sectors that are EARLY ENTRY. Skip NEUTRAL. The DECELERATING sector (Industrials) is where the institutional positioning tells you to lean small short.

Looking at this table I keep telling myself we are in late-cycle euphoria. But I have called late-cycle three times in the past two years and been wrong twice. So I do not size as if I am certain. I size as if the call is sixty-forty in my favor.

Volatility surface — the curve is asking for protection

The volatility complex is where the most underappreciated information of the week sits. The headline VIX number is calm at seventeen point one. The financial press looks at that and concludes the market is calm. They miss the structure entirely.

The three-month VIX is trading nineteen percent above the front-month VIX. That is the curve shape dealers run when they want to be long protection sixty days out and let the front-month decay into them. It is not a panic signal. Front-month vol is calm because nothing has happened yet. It is a positioning signal: the people who price options for a living are quietly accumulating downside protection at strikes they think will matter in two months. They have access to the same data we do, plus order flow we never see. When their curve says "buy further-dated puts", that is information.

Other volatility readings worth tracking this week
VIX (front month)21.51LIVEVIX3M21.82LIVEVIX3M / VIX1.014LIVEVARIANCE RISK PREMIUM+6.77 ptsSTATICSKEW INDEX136 (z=−1.65)STATICVVIX (vol of vol)ELEVATEDSTATICOVX (oil VIX)QUIETSTATICGVZ (gold VIX)QUIETSTATICVXN (Nasdaq VIX)MATCHES VIXSTATIC

The variance risk premium is positive six point seven seven points. That is the spread between what the options market is pricing (implied volatility) and what the underlying is actually delivering (realized volatility). When this spread is positive by seven points, the options market is paying you to sell premium on stretched names. For a trader running a hedged book this week, that means the cost of buying VIX call spreads is offset partially by the credit available from selling premium on euphoric mega-cap names. The net cost of the hedge is lower than the sticker price suggests.

The SKEW index sits at one hundred thirty-six with a one-year z-score of minus one point six five. Translation: the options market is not pricing tail risk. Out-of-the-money put protection is cheap relative to its own history. That is interesting because it means the institutional hedging activity I described in the third reading (XLI puts at twelve times open interest) is happening at relatively cheap premium. The hedgers are getting a deal. That is exactly when smart money tends to size up.

The implication for the week. The volatility surface is whispering "something is coming, but the market does not know it yet". The hedge cost is low. The premium income from selling on euphoric names is high. The smart money is positioning. If you are running a book this week, the volatility complex is doing half the work for you on the hedging side. You just have to listen.

I have to remind myself to look at the curve before sizing. Most weeks the curve is uneventful and I skip it. The weeks I skip it are usually the weeks I should not have. This is one of those weeks.

The top of this list is where the real institutional capitulation lives. Cardinal Health, Carvana, Datadog, Dell, Intel, McKesson, Motorola, NXP Semi all show up with score four or higher. What the historical pattern actually suggests, in detail

Six instances is a small sample, and I want to spend a moment on what each one looked like in real time so you can judge for yourself whether the pattern is meaningful or whether I am cherry-picking dates.

When the signal firedIndex 60 days laterWorst drawdown along the wayWhat happened
February 2020−33%−33%COVID exogenous shock
August 2020−8%−10%Mega-cap tech reset
October 2021−7%−12%Meme stock peak rolled over
March 2024−5%−7%Quiet top, fast resolution
July 2024−8%−10%Yen unwind catalyst
March 2025−19%−19%Tariff exogenous shock

The pattern that emerges from these six instances is that the signal does not predict crashes. It predicts pullbacks. The two outliers in the set — February 2020 and March 2025 — were tail events that turned ordinary pullbacks into thirty and twenty percent declines because of an exogenous shock. Without the shock, the historical resolution would be a five to ten percent pullback over sixty to ninety days, with the bottom typically reached between day thirty and day sixty. That is the base case I am positioning for this week.

I want to be honest about the limits of this analysis. Six observations is not enough to declare a hundred percent certainty about anything. What it is enough for, in my judgment, is to adjust posture. Six for six on direction with a defensible mechanical explanation is enough to tilt the book in the direction of the historical resolution, while keeping enough flexibility to be wrong about timing.

The other side, in detail (because the bear case can be wrong)

If I am only listening to the data that confirms what I already believe I am not doing real analysis. So let me lay out the bull case as honestly as I can, and tell you what would change my mind.

The strongest argument against my read is the breadth I described earlier. Seventy-five percent of sectors above their fifty-day average is not a tape that is breaking. Eight new highs and zero new lows is not a tape that is rolling over. The Hindenburg Omen is dormant. The McClellan Summation is positive. None of these readings are signaling distress.

The financial stress index sits in the bottom quartile of its multi-year range. There is no banking stress, no funding crisis brewing, no credit pressure. Every major drawdown in recent memory has been accompanied or preceded by some kind of credit stress. We do not have that here.

And there is the simple argument that respected macro traders like Paul Tudor Jones have publicly said the rally has another year or two to run before the cycle turns. People who have been in this business longer than I have, with bigger portfolios than mine, see the same data I see and reach a different conclusion. They might be right. They have been right before. I have been wrong before. The honest answer is that I do not know who is right, and the discipline of being wrong about the macro should be built into the position sizing, not relied upon to be avoided.

What would change my mind this week
If the equal-weight index outperforms the headline index by more than 1% in any single session, the rotation is starting and I would unwind the short side. If the financial stress index ticks up materially, the plumbing is starting to crack and I accelerate the defensive posture. If the variance risk premium collapses to zero or negative (realized vol catches up to implied), I close the hedge because the catalyst has arrived. None of these signals would be ambiguous.
Zoetis — the long anecdote

I want to spend a few paragraphs on Zoetis specifically because it is the trade I am most confident about for this week, and the path that brought me to it illustrates how I think about position selection in a defensive macro setup.

Zoetis is the global leader in animal health. The company spun out of Pfizer in 2013 and has been one of the most consistent compounders in the healthcare sector since. The business is straightforward. They sell vaccines, diagnostics, and medications for pets and livestock. The pet side has been the growth engine, driven by the long secular trend of pet ownership in developed markets. The livestock side is mature and stable, providing a consistent cash flow base.

Over the past four months the stock has been hammered. The narrative driving the drawdown is that pet ownership growth is decelerating in the post-COVID hangover, that consumer spending on vet care is softening, and that the company will need to guide down on the next earnings call. The stock is now down more than thirty percent from its prior high, the fifty-day average is rolling over, and the relative strength index just hit six point four. That is the lowest reading I have seen on a quality large-cap in this cycle. It is a once-a-cycle event.

Why ZTS is the cleanest setup in the dashboard this week
RSI 6.4 (lowest in the S&P 500). Capitulation triplet firing. Implied volatility below realized volatility (cheap premium). Multi-screener confluence list: ZTS at the top with 10 of 30 independent screens flagging the same name simultaneously. Insider buying in the most recent Form 4.

What makes the setup interesting is not just the technical capitulation. It is the fact that ten of thirty independent screens are flagging the same name simultaneously. The capitulation triplet is firing, the implied versus realized volatility anomaly is firing (the options market is pricing the stock as quiet while the realized movement is large), the multi-screener confluence list has it at the top, and the meta-confluence aggregator scores it as the highest priority name this week. When that many independent indicators converge on the same name, the probability that they are all wrong simultaneously is low.

The historical analog I am using is the 2018 healthcare quality capitulation. Several quality healthcare names hit similar capitulation footprints in the fall of 2018. The narrative at the time was that drug pricing pressure, regulatory risk, and competitive dynamics would compress margins for years. None of that turned out to be true. The names recovered to new highs over the following twelve months, with the typical move being twenty to thirty percent in the first ninety days. Zoetis specifically traded a similar setup in mid-2022 and again in early-2023, with similar resolutions.

The narrative driving the current drawdown is real. Pet ownership growth is genuinely decelerating from the COVID-era peak. Consumer spending on premium vet care is genuinely softening in the consumer slowdown. These are real concerns and I am not dismissing them. What I am saying is that the price action implies a structural earnings cut that the company has not actually guided to. The narrative is real but cyclical, not structural. Pet ownership in developed markets is a secular trend, not a cyclical one. The company will work through the consumer softness over the next few quarters and the earnings will recover. The stock is pricing in something more permanent than what the data actually supports.

That gap between price and reality is what creates the opportunity. If you only had bandwidth for one trade this week, this is the one I would choose. I will buy the stock at current levels or on a small further dip. Stop just below the recent swing low. First target at the fifty-day average from below. Holding period thirty to sixty days.

What I keep coming back to with this name is the asymmetry of the setup. If I am wrong about the capitulation footprint and the stock continues lower, the stop is roughly five percent below entry, which means I lose maybe two percent of the position. If I am right and the stock mean-reverts to the fifty-day average, the first target is plus twelve percent. If the second target gets hit at plus twenty-five percent, the trade is a five to one reward versus risk. With a probability of mean-reversion that I would put around fifty-five percent based on the historical analogs, the expected value of the trade is comfortably positive even if the timing takes longer than expected.

The other thing I want to flag about this name is the option market behavior. Implied volatility on the front-month options is below the realized volatility of the underlying. That sounds technical but the implication is straightforward. The options market is pricing the stock as if it is going to be quiet for the next month, while the actual price action over the past three weeks has been anything but quiet. When implied volatility prices a stock as boring while the realized movement is large, there is structural value in long-gamma plays. You can buy the call options cheaply relative to what the underlying is actually doing. For traders who prefer options to equity, this is the kind of setup where a long-dated call can be more efficient than the equity itself.

I keep asking myself whether this is one of those once-a-cycle setups or whether I am pattern-matching on the analogs that worked and ignoring the ones that did not. The honest answer is I do not know yet. I am sizing it small enough that being wrong is fine and large enough that being right matters.

Useful as a contrarian short watch list, but I do not trade individual short squeezes from this screen because the timing is too unpredictable for a defensive book.

The portfolio update — wins and stops

I publish the wins and the stops every week because the stops matter more than the wins for long-term credibility. Anybody can show you their winners. The discipline of showing you the losers, week after week, is what separates a real trading journal from a marketing exercise.

Last week notes leaned into the energy rotation theme and flagged a pair of capitulation candidates trading below their fifty-day average with smart-money interest. The energy basket finished the week roughly flat to slightly positive. The dip-buy ideas printed small green, nothing spectacular. Neither was a home run, neither was a disaster, both fit the thesis. That is the kind of week I aim for in a defensive regime. Small wins, no losses, dry powder preserved for when the better setups arrive.

Two trades from earlier in the year are worth updating. The dip-buy I sized into in late April is still open, currently up nine percent with the trail stop moved to break-even. I am holding it into the first target zone with a clean structural stop underneath. This trade has worked exactly the way I planned it, which happens rarely enough that I want to acknowledge it when it does.

The other trade from earlier in the year is the momentum position I added two weeks ago. It hit its stop on Tuesday, down six percent. Closed. The trade was correct at entry by every screen I use. The setup was clean, the macro context was supportive, the risk-reward was favorable. What changed was the macro context itself. The aggression ratio I have been tracking moved from neutral to extreme within ten days, and momentum trades stop working when the macro shifts faster than the position can absorb. That is the cost of being honest about timing. The trade was not a mistake. The mistake would have been to widen the stop or double down to avoid taking the loss.

Cumulative for the year, the book is up eight point four percent after fees, with a Sharpe ratio of one point three two and a maximum drawdown of about four percent. Nothing exceptional. The point of the system is not exceptional returns. The point is consistent positive expected value with managed downside, week after week, year after year. The exceptional returns come from compounding the consistency over time, not from any single trade.

How this ends, in the longer run

I want to spend the last few paragraphs on a longer-term thought, because the short-term tactical positioning I have laid out only makes sense in the context of where I think this cycle is heading.

I do not think this is a top in the same sense that 2007 or 2000 were tops. The companies driving this rally are real businesses with real cash flows generating real earnings. The technology powering the rally is delivering measurable productivity gains. The macro backdrop is not deteriorating the way it was in late 2007 or early 2000. There is no obvious credit stress brewing. There is no obvious recession around the corner. The conditions for a major bear market are not in place, and I do not think they will be in place for at least another twelve months.

What I do think is that within a longer bull market, you get periodic five to ten percent pullbacks driven by sentiment and positioning getting too one-sided. The data this week describes exactly that kind of setup. The leaders are stretched, the concentration is extreme, the dealer positioning is hedged, and the historical pattern when these conditions converge is a sixty to ninety day pullback in the leaders that allows the median to catch up. After the pullback resolves, the bull market typically resumes with broader participation and a new set of leaders that were previously laggards.

If that is the right read of the cycle, then the trade for this week is not "short the market". The trade is "trim the leaders, rotate to the laggards, hedge the book, and be ready to add aggressively to the names that pull back into the capitulation zone". That is the framework. The book this week is small short bias on the most stretched mega-caps and the sector with the largest institutional put accumulation, small long on the laggards already in capitulation (Zoetis, Cardinal Health, Energy via USO), and a VIX call spread hedge sized at one and a half percent of book.

That is the asymmetry I am looking for in defensive regimes. Limited downside if I am wrong, meaningful upside if I am right, and a clear set of signals that would tell me to change posture if the data shifts. None of this is exotic. None of this is original. It is just disciplined application of a system that has worked over time, applied to this week specific data.

One last thought before I close. The single biggest determinant of long-term trading performance is not signal generation. It is sizing discipline. I see traders all the time who have correct views on the market, who pick the right names, who time the entries reasonably well, and who still underperform because they oversize the high-conviction trades and undersize the medium-conviction trades. The sizing matters more than the picks because the sizing is what determines whether a single mistake takes the book down by five percent or by twenty-five percent.

This week is exactly the kind of week where sizing discipline matters most. The macro signal is high conviction, but the historical sample is small enough that I cannot be certain. The right response to that combination is medium sizing. Not the maximum size you would put on if you were certain, not the minimum size you would put on if you were uncertain. Something in the middle, with the option to add if the data confirms over the next two weeks, and the willingness to cut if the data shifts. That is what defensive bias actually looks like in a position sheet. Not aggressive shorts, not maximum hedge, just a careful tilt away from the leaders and toward the laggards, sized in proportion to a conviction that is high but not certain.

The other thing I want to say about this kind of regime is that the watch list matters more than usual. In a trending market with broad participation, you can set the book and forget it for a few days. In a regime where the macro is stretched and the leaders are concentrated, the conditions can shift fast. The aggression ratio I have been tracking can compress from plus three sigma back to plus one sigma in two weeks if the median catches up. The dispersion can close in either direction. The institutional positioning that took twelve times standing open interest to put on can unwind in a single session if the catalyst the institutions were hedging against fails to materialize. So the trades are sized for that optionality. They are not sized as if the next sixty days are deterministic.

Closing — and a question for you · Friday May 15, 2026

This setup remains the highest-conviction macro position I have taken in nine weeks. The data has been pointing this direction for three weeks straight, and the funnel keeps surfacing quality long candidates in the deep-value capitulation buckets at the same time the macro picture is most extreme. Zoetis and Cardinal Health in particular are the kind of capitulation names that pay handsomely when they show up alongside the macro thesis.

Reply to this email and tell me — are you defensive this week, or do you trust the breadth and stay long? I read every reply, and the best ones make their way into next week note. If you are defensive, what specifically convinced you. If you are long, what would have to break for you to reduce exposure. I learn more from the replies than from any single piece of data on my dashboard.

🎭 MEME IDEA — Spider-Man pointing (3 men): Three Spider-Men labeled "Bull (trusts breadth)", "Bear (trusts macro extremes)", "Cash on sidelines (trusts neither, waits for confirmation)". Caption: "The third Spider-Man wins more years than either of the first two."

One more thing on the meta. I write this newsletter every Sunday morning before the futures open. The discipline of writing it out forces me to articulate what I see and to be honest when the data and my gut disagree. There have been weeks where I sat down to write a defensive note and the act of laying out the data made me realize the macro signal was thinner than I had thought. There have been weeks where I sat down to write a bullish note and the act of laying out the data made me realize the bull case rested on a single line of momentum that could break at any time. The newsletter is not just a deliverable for readers. It is a forcing function for me. If I cannot defend the position in writing, I do not put it on. That is the real value of writing every week, even on the weeks where the macro picture is murky and the signals are mixed.

If you find yourself agreeing with the bear case but unable to actually trade against the index, that is not a personal failure. The mechanical loop I described earlier is genuinely strong, and the cost of being early to a turn that takes longer than expected can be substantial. The right answer for many readers will be to do nothing this week. Stay in cash, watch the data, wait for the resolution to start before adding exposure. There is no rule that says you have to trade every week. Sometimes the best trade is no trade. The book I described above is for traders who want defined exposure to the macro view. For everyone else, the right answer is patience and a smaller exposure than feels comfortable.

Trade safe. Size for the worst case before you bet on the best one.
